Pet lover here. Also mom to 2 children. Dogs, cats, purebreds, rescues.  Had an English Mastiff purebred. She was a real drooler. Laid back. Was the runt, at most weighed 170. Vet said she was a bit overweight for her size. Got her down to 150, perfect. She lived to be 12 yo. before we finally had to have her euthanized. She'd developed cardiomyopathy (common in large breeds), lost hearing and losing eyesite, incontinent. Vet was shocked she lived so long. Also, keep in mind, rescues have excess baggage and takes time to help them overcome with lots of love and patience. Also, cat hairball vomiting! Corgis are not chill. They are a herding breed and they want a job. Each dog is an individual and also shaped by their environment, but you frequently get what you breed for.  Reputable rescues will try to match you with a dog that works with your personality and lifestyle, and will usually insist that you return the dog to the rescue if it's not working out, rather than letting people dump them at animal control. All the rescues we've adopted from have been super cool with potential adopters having several get to know you visits with the dog before committing to anything. Also, you can spend one on one time with dogs at the shelter before making your decision. Some shelters will also allow you to foster the dog before adoption to make sure that its the perfect match before signing the adoption papers. Hope this helps ğŸ™‚

I used to own a Borzoi, and it is such a lovely breed. I'm surprised they're not more popular, but at the same time I'm kind of thankful for it, becuase I don't like when a breed suddenly grows "popular" because of a movie, meme or a celebrity owning it and such. Borzoi's grow up to be very big dogs, and they're a pretty independent breed who can have a bit of a stubborn streak, so they're not the most beginner friendly dogs despite their very sweet and gentle nature. They're not suitable for everyone. They really are lovely though. Full throttle when they're outside and running, and really affectionate couch potatoes when you take them inside and snuggle up in front of the TV. So graceful yet goofy at the same time, and they rarely bark. My boy was named Aristo - He got his name from one of the main characters of the book that the breeders daughter had written. I lost him to cancer in December 2022, and my mom lost her Husky Ayla in spring 2023 to old age, but just a few days ago my mom, my brother and I visited a litter of borzoi puppies and booked two of them, so at the end of this month we will have not just one borzoi in the family but two~ I still miss Aristo like crazy. He was all I could've ever wished for in a dog and more, so he will always have a very special place in my heart, but my mom, my brother and I have all really missed having a dog. We all live apart, but we used to take turns taking care of both Ayla and Aristo. Basically the one who had the least work or didn't have work that day took care of the dogs, and if more then one of us were free then we would often take the dogs out on some small adventure or take them to the dog park and play. My family has always had dogs ever since I was little and loved spending our time with them. The only one who has a dog in my family right now is my older sister. She has an American staffordshire terrier, or Amstaff as we call them here in Sweden. She's a very sweet dog, but not really my cup of tea. She's a bit too wild and spoiled in my opinion. My mom jokingly says that these two little ones are my fault since I was the one introducing my family to Borzoi's and made them fall in love with the breed ^^  My mother booked a female. She's black and tan with a bit of white, and she has the most adorable little freckles on her nose. They almost look like a four-leaf clover. My babybrother and I are buying her brother together. He's stunning~ I'm not a 100% sure what his color counts as. I think he would count as white with fawn spots? He has very long legs and big paws, so I think he'll grow up to be quite a big boy. Probably a late comment, but for finding the right dog from a rescue I would recommend seeing if they have a foster-to-adopt system. The rescue I work for does that. If you don't want to commit to fostering, in case it doesn't work out, we also do sleepovers. The animal would already have a foster, so you have the animal for whatever amount of time you agreed upon and if it doesn't work out they go back to their foster. You could also see if the foster is comfortable with a meet-and-greet at their home to get a feel for how the animal acts on familiar turf. Several options to make sure personalities gel before you commit.

Bread
I know for my family and I, when it comes to looking for rescue animals, we don't really go out actively looking for them- they seem to find us. Somehow when we have a space to fill in our house(or maybe need to make a space we didn't know we had), there's been a dog or cat looking for a home.  And what's wonderful about rescue animals is that they are just so grateful to have a home, that they have always made the most loving pets. Sometimes they have problems because of past abuse or not being trained well as a puppy, but you get to work on it together and bond with them in the process. Plus you grow as a person too. So in my case it has been a total win-win. ğŸ˜Š

I can attest to the healthy weight dogs live longer. My grandpa over fed 2 of his dogs and they died before they were 9.  On the other had, my dog was 17 year old chocolate lab earlier this week when she had to be put down. She was 17! She got fed twice a day, got treats when she came to the door after being let out (I live in the country), liked walks before he hips got arthritis, and loved eating lettuce, carrots, and apples. She weighed 60ish pounds most of her life. My mom saw her wandering on the side of the road near my house, asked her if she wanted to go for a ride, and brought her home. When no one responded to the found dog signs we put up we decided to keep her and she had been with us ever since. We honestly were expecting her to die about 5  year ago when her hips started to pain her.  So yes, keeping your dog at a healthy weight is a factor in having them with you for a long time. If you choose to adopt a rescue, visit the rescue place a few times first. Walk some dogs, play with a few cats, introduce anyone you get along with to Bear to make sure they get along with each other as well. If you find a dog that walks nicely with you and Bear, and someone that seems to fit your lifestyle, that's who you (fill out an adoption application to) take home. If you rent your home, they may need your landlord or HOA approval first.   Adoptions usually ask for references, and I like putting down our veterinarian because he volunteers at the at the local shelters sometimes on weekends to spay/neuter their newbies. I figure there's no one they'd trust more.  Rescues are awesome. Our Lhasa Apso spent about half an hour digging and dancing around in his dog bed when we first got him home. I never saw a dog so GRATEFUL for a cushion before.

I adopted an adult rescue dog end last year and it's been very different from getting a puppy but not all bad. Here's the quick version:  Selection: I contacted the shelter manager and told her roughly what I was looking for: basically a dog that was gentle enough to put up with my existing terrier cross breed, small to medium size, any breed, preferably male, tho it doesn't really matter, good with cats as there is one in one half of the house. I also told her I'm happy to take one with minor health issues since those are usually difficult to find homes for. I told her I have stairs so the dog needs to be OK with that. She sent me photos of about 12 dogs that matched my request, with a special focus on the ones with minor health issues as its rare for them to get people interested in taking that on.  I made an appointment to go see the dogs. After meeting each one, one of them stood out to me. Whereas the others were quite content, this guy really seemed to need some extra love. He was extremely soft natured, and I've had experience dealing with that. I went back and visited my top 3. By the time I was done visiting there was no doubt in my mind. There was one other dog at the shelter that caught my eye (I tried to put on blinkers coz it's so hard for me to not want to take them all home, so basically went in with my heart hardened with determination: just one, think clearly). He wouldn't have got on with my existing dog as they have Similar personalities. If he is there when my old boy goes, I'll go back for him. If not then he's found a home and I'm sure is happy.  Initial take away: a lot of rescues go through a lot of emotions during the rehoming process. They leave everything they know and are put in situations where we expect them to be fine, but it takes them time to adjust. You have to give them lots of time and reassurance. Lots. Introduce one new thing at a time: ie, new home environment. Only when they're comfy with that, do u add the next thing: driving in car, meeting family, etc. They will show signs of distress in ways that u might not realise, ie, panting a lot. It really helps if the shelter owner can give u a little talk about things to expect etc. It also helps if they can match the suggested dogs personality to yours somewhat. Ie you would want a dog that's a bit more self confident, for example. Also one that has been fostered in a home environment before might help. Mine was a shelter boy all 6 years of his life, so everything was a huge change for him.   Pros: Adult dogs learn faster than puppies. They don't need to learn through trial and error, tho they may still take chances - but you can just be firm and they usually respect the boundary. He is actually better trained wrt handling than my own dog - let's me clip all his nails, does as told, let's me brush him, wash him, etc. This was done regularly by a staff of volunteers at the shelter. It has made owning him soooo much easier.   Tip : Adopt at a time when you have a little extra time available to be there for them. They will need plenty of reassurance and comforting. It helps the bonding process a lot if you can be around to comfort them when they're stressed.  With having another pet, u might want to arrange to take bear out to meet his new potential siblings and see if he has any preferences. They don't always like to share their humans or home.   Shelter dogs appreciate everything about life in a home - couches, food, cuddles, walks, games. It's not just a way to be with their human, it's like a little dog experience all on its own. They give you their love way before you've figured out what makes them tick.  The hardest part has been forming a bond. I feel like it's less like having a child and more like making a friend. It takes a bit of time for you to both get to know each other. But the love does come - you just have to try not to think about it and enjoy the experience. The more you get to know each other, the more the love comes. We're 5 months in and it's already starting to feel really good. We appreciate each other quite a lot. We are both big cuddlers  Biggest con: he doesn't like sharing me, and he gets stressed when I'm away later than usual. However he is tolerant and well behaved about both. I just have to have a hand on each of my boys when they want cuddles simultaneously. Also he's a bit unaware of himself around the older dog, so I often have to make sure he's not bumping him over accidentally etc.  Oh, and he's an incredibly good watch dog too! Only barks when something strange is going on, and can hear a pin drop when it comes to that.  That's my experience. Hope it helps! A kitty might actually be great with bear. Bear is a tolerant dog and a cat wouldn't impeed your relationship. They are also usually quite low maintenance - which would be a plus with such a high maintenance dog! Not sure how the cat will get on with you tho... Dogs tend to be more tolerant of vibrant personality types ğŸ˜…ğŸ˜‚

An older rescue may take a long time and energy to rehabilitate, and then they may never rehabilitate depending on their trauma. I'm more of a cat person, but in my experience with shelters, most shelters I know of will allow you to spend some time with their animals before you make a decision. So I'd go with some time to spare, let the shelter workers know what practical things I can accomodate, and go spend some time with the dogs. I always end up finding and falling in love with a pet there. Once I came home with two when I meant to get one, but it felt right to keep them together. A lot of the rest is just the two of you learning to co-habitate, the same as any roomie or family member. I think several shelters let you walk dogs too, so if you're very active with your dog, you might be able to arrange to take some dogs you've met on a run with you and see how they react to exercise.

On adopting a shelter dog; it's difficult because many of them have anxiety. They were abandoned by the people they trusted most, and they struggle to adjust, but if they have another calmer dog (like Bear) they tend to adapt well.  The best way to determine a good fit for you is: 1. Talk to the old person that's been at that shelter for 10+ years. They know their stuff. 2. Ask the staff about their favorites. Vice-versa ask them about their least favorite, and why. 3. See if you can do a trial period. You may end up with a dog Bear loves, but that same animal is terrified of you or genders (based on scent; my family had a dog that hated men in hats, but loved all women). It was weird. 4. There's no fail-safe way to find a rescue fit, BUT there is a guarantee your hous or renters insurance will drop you with certain breeds in your home. No joke. Call your insurance.  5. Get a mutt. No joke. The varied breeding unlocks other breeds personality traits, but that's the best part. My mutt looks like an inflated chihuahua that got confused and married a corgi, but she has a singing voice like a beagle.  There's no fool proof way to guarantee a good match. Kennel training a shelter dog is usually easier if they've been at the shelter for very long at all. You want one that's been there a minute. They've usually already received some training from staff.  That's all I can think of. âœŒï¸

When you find a dog, it usually doesn't matter on their past as I'm sure you would give them the time they need to settle. Rescues are usually quite jumpy but mainly calm and cuddly. They just want attention and love.

if you want to adopt a rescue, i recommend an older dog because they often have a harder time finding a home. to see if a dog fits your personality, i recommend going to the shelter and bringing ones that mean a lot to you. such as friends, family, or other pets. if the dog gets along with the other people you care about, it is likely that you will have a good bond with them. also, watch them interact around the shelter and watch their body language to see if they are generally chill or anxious, hyper or tired, and so on. hope this helps!

My one tip on adopting a dog from a rescue: Be sure to give yourself time to interact with the dog! When I adopted my puppy I spent three hours at the rescue just sitting with all the puppies and getting to know their personalities. This is even more important with adult dogs, since they have more developed behaviors already. With our adult dog, we visited with her outside, inside, and then had her on a trial period for a week. Many rescues offer this, and I think its a great idea to see how the dog will fit into your household, particularly with other pets. But also: don't ignore behavioral warning signs! We briefly had a puppy who had an incredibly strong prey drive, and wasn't really able to bond well with humans. Essentially, she was like a fluffy golden wolf. Looking back, we should have noticed the signs. (Not looking at our faces, not playing with the other puppies, extremely tense when other dogs barked nearby etc.) So for sure watch out for those signs. One last thing: Adopting a puppy is kind of like shopping for fancy clothes. You have to assert yourself, or you will end up with something that just won't work out. People who volunteer or work at animal shelters are amazing individuals, doing and awesome service for the community. They really care about their animals and they REALLY WANT TO FIND THEM HOMES. You may encounter some pressure to adopt the dog, and you will probably get pushback if you express behavioral concerns. But all in all rescue dogs are wonderful, and I'm sure there's one out there for you! <3

Hey Dr. Mike  I volunteered for a well established rescue for 5 years during adoption events.  These are a few considerations and thinking points. Some are the hard points to consider when looking at all those sweet faces.   Take Bear so if there is a candidate you can see how Bear and Rescue interact.    Determine energy level and size range you are interested in.  Puppy or adult? Make list of excluding traits.    The hard stuff: Recognize that if a dog has been living with other people (so it is age 6 months or older) there may be behaviors that have to be learned before training in those areas starts or possibly redirect the behavior.   Recognize that it will change the dynamic you have with Bear.  Bear may not change as much as some dogs do.   Consider the activities such as hiking and what if the rescue has to take more time to learn good behavior in those situations.   There are potential issues of the other dog has food or toy guarding tendencies.  The rescue should be able to inform you of that.   If a rescue tells you all the good things about several dogs and no behaviors that could be considered difficult beware.  Each of our dogs has their â€œAs is clause (claws?)â€. We wanted people to have the full scope of the dog, not just the warm, floofy feelings part.   Wishing you the best

Honestly, having a rescue of my own, it does benefit if you figure out why they became a rescue dog in the first place. Sometimes it could be that they had a bad experience with a nother, too much yelling, being left alone, or, what we think happened to my rescue, abuse or harsh treatment from the man of the house. My rescue, Julius, is a very active dog and never wants to put the toy down, he always wants someone to play with him and throw the ball. When dogs repeatedly bring the toy to their owner, from what I've read, that's their way of giving and receiving affection to and from their owner. Rescues also are, a lot of times, eager to please to their owner so they tend to listen better as to not get their owners bad side. Julius does calm down when everyone else is calm and when it gets later in the day. Research the dog's breed, or mix of, could also help when looking for dogs that fit your personality. Julius is a Boston terrier, like all the dogs my family has had, and they're great people and kid friendly. They also are very loyal and protective. Whenever my dogs hear the doorbell they immediately start barking but after realizing the person at the door isn't a threat they calm down. Julius, who is full Boston terrier unlike the other two of mine, is also a hunter. He'll lead you right to his toy if he can't get it. Julius even found a vole in our garden one year and scared it off. He's not allowed in the garden normal but when he kept going back we found out why and then he stayed out of the garden. Don't know if you'll read this, but if you do, I hope this helps.

A lot of dog rescues are typically specific to one breed or a size of dog (xs, s, m, l, xl/xxl) and they foster their dogs at volunteer's homes and the organization will match you with a dog that fits your personality and your lifestyle or whatever you're looking for. Going to shelter is a bit of gamble with dogs because they don't spend as much time with their dogs as they would like or be able to, and they don't get to see the dogs in a non-shelter environment. However, adoption is faster and easier. Rescues typically require a recommendation, house visit, past veterinarian information, etc. and are more expensive. The whole thing can takes months but if you're really set on a specific breed and wanting to find one that fits your specific lifestyle, then it's the best way to go.

doctor mike. when you get a rescue you need to be very aware of their mentality and history. a lot of rescues i have seen have some form of ptsd, anxiety, and depression as a lot of them i have seen were rescued from abuse. if you get an animal that was abused it may be afraid of people. animals will hold that trauma and not let anyone near them. they will struggle to trust you which would make it very hard to groom them, and take care of any other needs that would require being close to them. just like in a person it would take a lot of work to help them, through the trauma and in some they may never recover from it depending on personality.

pet finder
I love corgis but if you are planning on getting one, you should know they are not "chill" dogs. They are herding dogs and need something to do. This is especially true for Pembroke Corgis.  Pembroke Corgis need 1-2 hours of exercise a day.  Cardigan Corgis are less energetic but still high energy. They do tend to calm down the older they get but when they are younger, they need daily exercising. They also tend to be bossy in that they try to herd other dogs around them  which is ok if Bear doesn't mind it. If you think Bear may get annoyed with that behavior, maybe foster a Corgi first to see if you and Bear are compatible. I use to really want a corgi also but then a friend of mine fostered one and that little bundle of energy never stopped moving. Corgis are sweet,  intelligent, and cute as a button but "chill" they are not. If you are looking for a dog that is low energy plus is good for anxiety and depression, pugs, great danes and King Charles spaniels are all good options. English bulldogs are a good option also but they do come with health issues but if you have the financial means to take care of the, they are really wonderful and loyal dogs.

If you do decide to buy a shelter dog instead of buying from a breeder, just avoid pitbulls. Pits are mostly what you will find in the shelter. They were originally bred to fight other pitbulls in a pit, and attack chained bulls and bears. Hence the name, Pitbull. No amount of love or training removes their genetics. This is why they kill people and pets so often, despite being raised from puppies with only love. They are unpredictable and will kill their owner. Their is a myth all man biters were culled, but that just isnâ€™t true. Obviously.   Unfortunately there is a lot of propaganda out there that they are nanny dogs (myth created in 1971) or great family dogs. They are good catch dogs when hunting hogs or coyotes, and of course great at fighting which is illegal now.   So unless your using it to catch aggressive prey, and you have no children, and no other dogs, look for another breed.   I recommend to just buy from a good breeder. They screen the parent dogs and puppies for health problems, breed good temperament, and the puppy will have no unknown history like shelter dogs. And you get to choose the breed that has temperament that works for you. Not everyone needs a border collie, or hound dog, etc.   Shelters will actually adopt out pitbulls and dogs that have killed people, or bitten them unfortunately. Itâ€™s best to avoid shelters.

Usually, shelters allow you to take one of their dogs out into an enclosed area where you can play, interact and get to know them. It's usually really obvious what their personality is after these sessions. Shelter workers also usually will either know the personality themself, or their info page on their kennels will have a personality sections you can check.  Local shelters are the best usually, sometimes I try to find who's been there the longest and give them a shot â™¥

Show us your dog more often
I'd recommend going to local shelters from my experience if you don't like a challenge to maybe get a really difficult dog! :)  I'm getting my 4th dog on the 11th, 1 from the shelter, 3 from animal welfare. Only 2 I got to see before I bought them.  The first dog - Rocky, neglected in a household in France (we live in Germany) - was from our local shelter. We dropped some stuff we still had from our rabbits and guinea pigs, went to look at the dogs after and saw him. It was love at first sight, asked if we could go into his cage, they agreed and he was really happy about it. We had to walk him a few times in order to get him, so we already had an idea about what he's like. Also all shelters have a description about the dogs (most of the time at least) that's mostly pretty accurate. The people from the shelter visited us a few weeks after to see if the dog was fine (also to mention: we knew the people working there for a long time, so they'll probably check before if they don't know you).  The second dog - Pepsi, thrown over the fence of a killing station in a plastic bag with her sisters, from Rome/Italy - was at a private persons house where she cared for a few dogs from different animal welfare organizations, some of them directly from a killing station.  We saw one we liked on the homepage from a local shelter and made an appointment. We kinda met "all" dogs (since they lived together) and could directly interact with them and another dog caught our attention. We could bring her home over the weekend to see how we could fit. We didn't get any visitation from anyone from the shelter before or after. Both of them were absolutely beautiful dogs! But Pepsi needed a lot of work since she was scared of men and any rustling bags. You'll never know what they had to endure.   Our third dog - Tia - is also from animal welfare. We didn't meet her before, we only saw pictures and had a description of her on Facebook. She was still in Bucharest/Romania when we bought her. Someone visited us beforehand and they decided if we're suitable to own a dog.  Once new owners are found, they drive them every Friday through Hungary, Austria and Germany. They have around 50-60 dogs in their transporter and we had to drive for 2hrs to get her, since they only stop every so often to drop off dogs (and cats!) to a handful of people at the same time. All the time from their start in Romania, through every stop, we got notifications about how the dogs are feeling and what time to get them (we had one big messenger group). We got a quick crash course of what to expect, how not to wash them right away, etc. But beware: Those descriptions aren't necessary true! I saw from the pictures she'll get bigger than they said (and she was even bigger than we thought when we saw her xD), but we couldn't judge her character. They wrote she's puppy-like. Playful and open. She wasn't but all ther siblings were. She's a really scared, shy dog (and you could tell from her pictures that she doesn't trust those people around her). Even after nearly 2 years she still is easy scared and it'll probably never change. She wasn't feeling well from the journey (it were over 24hrs for her) and we could only take her, if we had a transport box (otherwise a car/seat belt for dogs (idk how to call it in english) was fine).  People from welfare said a lot of dogs are given back and I kinda understand why. It's hard to make a proper introduction for everyone of them because there are sooo many and probably not enough people looking after them. Especially dogs from Romania where they treat them mostly just bad (even "normal" owners won't treat their dogs good) will all be kinda traumatized and it can be really hard. One of Tia's sisters bites and needs a muzzle now, a brother is also kinda aggressive and wants to do his own thing. On the other hand: Tia may be scared easily but she is a super sweet girl now that listend really good as long as nothing spooks her! But: If she would've been our first dog, we would've been totally overchallenged and probaly looked for someone who could handle her. It was hard even with experience from traumatized shelter dogs.  And the 4th will be from the same organization as Tia is (and they visited us beforehand again, but since they knew we already have a dog from them we were prioritized and the dog was already reserved before out meeting). We also have only pictures of him, but some where he playes with someone and overall really playful, interested looking photos. So probably not as scared as Tia. The woman saving him also told us he'll be playful and not scared. I still think he'll get a little bigger than they wrote but for me that's no problem. I mean.. you see the pictures, if you know your dogs breeds you kinda know how big they may get.  It can also be pretty hard to tell which race the dog is, so you probably won't get any idea of what his characteristics can be, what they need to be happy (eg a lot of work) or possible race-specific illnessess that could occur. Buying a dog over the internet and without being able to see them can be extremly hit or miss, so I recommend already having experience with more difficult dogs! As someone with experience with only proper treated dogs/puppies from breeders or privat, I'd choose local shelters, that make sure you meet them a few times to make sure you'll fit any day!

We work with people to find who is the best fit for them. We require our shelter dog to meet their dog to make sure they get along, and if all goes well we send them home :) Most shelters try to make it as easy as possible

RESCUE DOGS: If you know of a specific breed that you would like, there are rescue organizations specifically oriented towards most breeds. Shelters are always full of pitbulls; any other breeds are adopted by someone within a week.  Since you like less athletic dogs, I highly suggest looking into greyhounds. After about 2 years of racing they are abandoned or put down but they actually make great companions. While everyone associates them with being really fast runners and racing dogs, they are actually couch potatoes and are perfectly happy to laze around most of the day so long as they do get to go for a long walk or a quick sprint around a park or track every once in a while.

GET THE CATTT!!!!
As someone who works at a shelter... get him a cat. Just be sure to do a proper introduction and to slowly introduce the cat into the home and to your dog. There's a lot of guides to it (and I'm happy to explain it!), and there are always plenty of cats who need homes, especially during the warmer months and the horrid kitten season. You can also train gets and can walk them on a harness and even put them in cat backpacks. It's easier to start out with kittens, since they are more malleable, but there are adult cats who might be accustomed to such lifestyles and are less adverse to change. :)  That being said, good luck finding a solid brown cat. I believe it's the rarest colour in cats or certainly one of them. I have personally only ever seen a few pictures here and there. You could get a long or medium black cat pretty easily, though! Black cats still have a harder time getting adopted than any other colour.

I don't usually stop to like videos, but I really like the cat idea. I grew up with both cats and dogs, so I thought that was normal until I realized how many people are either dog or cat people. I think it would be great to show that you can be both (and to learn about any evidence-based best practices for helping them get along). Also, please ask LegalEagle about disclosing information to your dog (or anything really, because your last collabs were so great). :)
I have two rescues; one I got based on 'I need an older dog' (because I had a very old dog at that point and a young dog would have bothered her, so I adopted an 8-year-old) and the other I got almost purely on looks (reminded me of my old dog). They are the opposites of each other in every way, but somehow both exactly perfect for me. You never know 100% what you get with rescues, they can be very different in shelter conditions than how they are at home, and a lot of them also have trauma to heal from, so they'll change over time. Of course you can expect behaviours/temperaments based on breeds and of course if you get to meet the dogs beforehand that can help with the selection process. I think rescue dogs are great, they are no worse than other dogs, and by getting one you are helping that dog get a new life.

These videos are so wholesome ğŸ’• and Bear is so adorable! I previously worked 6 years in the veterinary field and have worked alongside many rescue organizations here in BC, Canada. Each rescue has different criteria/adoption processes, however, most require you to fill out an application form. In the form youâ€™ll describe yourself and or any other people living with you (and their ages, if thereâ€™s children) usually what your occupation is, living conditions (ie: apartment, house, acreage, fenced yard etc), what pets are also living in the home (if you do have pets; how they are with other animals etc), and what youâ€™re looking for with adoption (preference in breeds, age, sex and personality types). Once you submit the form, the organization will reach out to you to conduct an interview to confirm the information is correct and that you are a responsible individual to provide care for the animal etc. They will also work to help find compatible matches and eventually lead to the adoption process, which includes a meet and greet and usually a home trial. Hope this info helps â¤

You know what: Cat advice 1: Brown cats do exist but it's one of the rarer colours, I don't know if finding a longhair brown cat is realistic. Alternatively, longhair black cats frequently appear brown in the sun. This is probably the best path if you want â€˜Cat that looks like Bearâ€™, though if you just want them to look â€˜relatedâ€™ then colourpoint cats with brown faces are more common than complete brown.  2: As for the cat itself, one thing it will absolutely need is territory. Me and my dog just reacted to doctor Mike and his dog reacting to dog memes.   Also because Mike said he wants to get a rescue dog but doesn't know how to find one that matches him: honestly just go for it and meet a bunch of different dogs before you commit to one. My dog is a rescue. I got him off a website where they rescue them from the streets in Hungary and then bring them back to Austria where I live. On this website you could scroll through all the profiles of the dog with their information and character description to give you a first idea of which ones would be fitting to you. When I saw one that I really liked I called the number on the site and was able to meet him because he was staying with a family and a bunch of the other rescues in my city. At the meeting the dog seemed uninterested in me. Instead another one of the rescues sat right down on my lap like he already trusted me. When they told me that he is the shyest of them all because he only stays to himself, doesn't play with the other dogs and is afraid to be touched I knew he was the one. And when I watched his behaviour when he was among the others he did seem to be unhappy with where he was. I took him home and we've been inseperable ever since. We have a really strong mental connection. I always know what he's thinking and we help each other through our anxiety and trauma. So my biggest advice to anyone who's thinking about getting a pet has always been: let the animal choose you. Because they have a much higher sensitivity for these things. I had the luck to grow up always surrounded by many different animals. My family and I always let the animal choose us and it's worked wonders.

I recently adopted a dog from my local humane society. They required a form to be filled out prior to adoption which gives them a profile of our lifestyle and how a dog would fit into it best. They asked questions such as what type of activity level is important to us, how long would our dog need to be left alone on an average day, are we comfortable with a dog who has existing medical needs, etc. We honestly got the best dog too. She's an older dog, 4 years, but she has the most lovely calm personality and has already had some training before we adopted her so she knows a lot of commands. We put in applications on a number of different dogs before getting her. But I'm so glad we didn't match with any of the previous dogs, because my girl is just the best. I've had her a month now and I completely adore her.

I've got 2 rescues, both of whom are perfect matches for me! I didn't get either of them from shelters, though. So, I don't have experience with that. However, the reason I didn't go with shelter is for the same reason presented, I wanted enough time to make sure they were good matches.   In both cases, I sought out an independent, non-profit rescue instead of a big chain shelter like the Humane Society. In both cases, we got a lot of time to get to know the dog before bringing, first him then her, home.   My advice would be to do the same. Look for the opportunity to have a few "babysitting" sessions with the dog before committing. I don't know if shelters allow that, since I've never gone that route, but take more time than a few minutes while shopping. Take a weekend if you can. Visit them multiple times before deciding. That's my advice.

Most shelters (the good ones, like SPCA) will actually have you fill out an application and see your personality, housing, lifestyle, energy level, etc, and then they will interview you (maybe) if they have more questions. If you already have dog(s) in your household, they will actually want your dog(s) to be at the shelter (or another neutral place) to see how they interact with each other. The staff at the shelter will determine if the dog you like actually fits your lifestyle (or they will see if there's a dog that matches your lifestyle). For a chill, calm dog, I (personally) recommend trying a retired racing greyhound, or a retired service/guide dog (it will be a super long waitlist though cuz everyone knows these dogs are sooooo well-trained, and the adoption fee will be higher. If you live in NY, consider taking a look at Guiding Eyes of the Blind, speaking as a volunteer there), or maybe a retired military canine (but some of them can be triggered by car alarms/certain smells/ or some random things due to its military background, and some military dogs can bite to that trigger since they are trained when to bite, also a super long waitlist), or a senior dog (that has been in a shelter for a long time, but most likely to be a bully breed/pit mix). Hope it helps.

My cat:I,m a storm cloud and I,m a sassy girl/storm cloud
Best tip for shopping a rescue, really... Is ASK THE RESCUERS and other staff working the place. They'll know who's who and spend time with ALL of the animals in their care. If there's a likely "truly athletic and fun loving" or a "loud mouth but sweet heart", those people will know...      It's also REALLY worth their time and effort to be up front about everything. The last thing they want is a pissed off "customer" bringing back a dog that had some unexpected issues, especially behavioral ones. If you just want a chill dog, SAY SO... It's not difficult. I've worked the rescues and rehab's and most of us REALLY do not want to have to go retrieve a dog that was supposedly headed to a forever-home.      Other than that, it's just worth a little extra time to make MORE THAN ONE VISIT... This isn't exactly a place to hang out, but you don't get a dog (or any pet) quite the same way you go to Walmart and buy a claw hammer. Get some insight to the people around the place as much as you get their insights for the dogs or cats or whatever else. It helps to just be sure you're working with a group "on the up and up", and most of them generally are legit'. There are a few who can slip into the pitfalls of dealing with puppy mills and some dubious things, though... You might even consider occasional volunteer time to work with a group... AND while Dr. Mike might be too busy, I also KNOW more than Dr. Mike should see this... SO think about it. There's no better way to get to know a facility than to spend real time there... regardless of what they do.  ;o)

hi mike, i volunteer at dog shelters and as a dog trainer. my piece of advice is get the dog out of the shelter environment. countless wonderful dogs are looked over in shelters because their personality doesnâ€™t come out until they leave the stressful environment. I have a soft spot for nervous dogs, so I often walk them. let me tell you, the change i see in some of these dogs once they leave the shelter is remarkable. many shelters allow you to walk with the dog, which is a great opportunity to get to know the dog better! I always recommend patience. if you are interested in a particularly nervous dog, get on their level and avoid eye contact. treats are always a good idea if the shelter allows it. i suggest having an idea of what temperament of dog you would like, as deciding by breed is not always feasible. being open to breeds that often face prejudice, like pitbulls, staffies, and german shepherds help clear out shelters. i find those breeds and huskies to be the most common. as always, do your research! ask questions about the dogâ€™s history! and remember, a dog can change dramatically when in a loving home. most importantly, when you meet the right dog for you, you will know. they will steal your heart. rescuing is so important.

Okay, Doctor Mike, take a seat. 1.  Not a Whippet, dog is a Borzoi (formerly called Russian Wolfhound). And judging by that photo of the pure bread, 10 to 1 that is a Borzoi, too. And don't argue with me on this, I used to have one. 2. As far as adopting a dog, there are a lot of breed rescue organizations (there's even one for newfies). I'm probably going to get a lot of flack on this but, I believe in the preservation of pure breds. If someone wants to adopt a mixed breed, just know that's a 50/50 situation as one doesn't know what genetic traits (good or bad) of the ancestry is in that dog. I would recommend you do some research (oh no, more reading?) on the different breeds and groups. My Borzoi was in the Hound Group, Bear is in the Working Group. See what jumps out at you, then focus your reading on that breed. I recently adopted a 5 yo Belgian Tervuren and his personality is what I wanted in my next dog.  3. Yes, dogs do remember bad things, and if they're getting negative punishment, the whole experience will be ingrained in their psyche.  Pee whoop!

If you are looking into rescues, I just suggest being very patient with them. We rescued my most recent dog, she is a purebred pit-bull, and she was very aggressive with her food. If other dogs came near her food she would snap at them but after a few months of slowly bringing them into the same area for feeding, she stopped snapping. Depending on the breed of dog you get, the service you get them from, and their conditions from before they were rehabilitated, they can take longer to trust you. But give them time and they will eventually start to trust you.

Dear Dr. Mike,    Don't worry at all about rescuing animals. When you rescue a dog, especially while it's still young, it's personality adapts to yours as it grows. It becomes accustomed to your habits and preferences, filling a little puzzle-piece-shaped hole in your heart. Rescue animals seem to know that you are their savior, and end up adoring you ad infinitum for the rest of their lives.    Yours truly,      Laura Barsalou

I have volunteered in a rescue organization now for a decade and I totally believe you can find a perfect rescue dog for yourself. You don't have to do it all by yourself! The volunteers/staff of the shelters or rescue organizations know their dogs incredibly well and they will help you find the right match. People often contact us asking which cat or dog we believe would be best for them. We are here to help. This is how my friend found their Corgi/Golden retriever mix. I have had 2 dogs in my life one was a pure bread beagle, and the other was a rescue. I never thought my heart could heal after my beagle passed because he lived until he was 15, and he was my childhood dog that I loved. After a few months my family got my rescue dog, and he had a place in my heart. We got him as a puppy, and he was stand offish, but he was spoiled and loving. I felt safe emotionally and physically when he was with me. He never bit anyone, but he took time to warm up to a new person. He wouldn't just walk up to any stranger to let him pet him. He was a very high anxiety dog. He would also get nervous being in another person's care while we were gone which wasn't often, so I suggest getting that dog used to someone else caring for them too. He was well behaved, and constantly bark at the treat cabinet because he wanted treats. We had to put him down recently due to a spinal cord injury, but all he wanted to do was be with you, and be loved. We are planning on adopting from a rescue again because he was an amazing dog. I suggest looking at local shelters and meeting dogs to see what dog will fit best with your life style.

Rescue dog from a shelter: Like some others said, meet and greets (outside of the kennels, usually in a play yard), just remember they are in a shelter environment for a long period of time and may take time settling in the home regardless of what you see. If someone interests you they should allow you to bring Bear to meet them, too, but shelters and rescues vary a ton in their processes.  Rescue dog from a "rescue" that has no location and is usually a hodgepodge of volunteers: most often require a home visit and in my area have always required both dogs to meet. Maybe see if any offer foster to adopt which gives the dog some time in your home to see if it's the right fit.

For adopting another dog or cat, if youâ€™re going the rescue route hereâ€™s a few suggestions. Look up different rescues or even sanctuaries (some sanctuaries also adopt out) in and around your area, see what people say about them, what they do, if they put down over 90% of their animals like PETA does (run). If youâ€™re looking for a particular breed, many times there are rescues/sanctuaries that are dedicated to one specific breed. If you fall in love with a dog that maybe doesnâ€™t live that close to you, you can always make a day of it and make it a trip as well as going to adopt but thereâ€™s also people who will fly or drive your new friend home to you. You might need to pay something at least to cover gas costs, etc. Ask yourself what kind of dog would fit your and your dogâ€™s lifestyle. Bear looks like a chill dog so probably not something super hyper and active, a dog that doesnâ€™t bark every 2 seconds, or a very vocal cat, etc. You can ask about trial adoptions or foster to adopt so that you have a while to decide if this new pet will fit in with Bear. Make sure the animal comes with medical records; proof of testing, shots, a rabies certificate (if theyâ€™re above a certain age). Both cats and dogs usually have shots required by the state you live in so look that up.  Get your new friend vetted by a licensed veterinarian ASAP. Get the full blood work up especially testing for common and communicable diseases, stool analysis to check for worms and other parasites, a full physical to make sure skin and fur is free of fleas, ticks, mange, ringworm, etc, check the teeth see if any dental needs to happen. Keep your new pet in quarantine for at least a few days. The stress of being brought to a new home can lower the immune system of the new pet and make it more likely that they can acquire URI, UTI, or a cough and you donâ€™t want to spread that. Also Bear can stress as well and the same thing can happen where he can give something to the new pet. You know thereâ€™s a bigger chance of spreading infection dog to dog then cat to dog or dog to cat.

Okay first he knows not to get off the elevator because he reads your body language. Dogs are incredible when it comes to reading body language.  Next if you really want to adopt a dog but aren't sure if it will fit your lifestyle you can always ask questions. When it comes to shelter dogs questions are your best friend. Ask the staff to give you as much info as they can on that dog. Why was it released to them? How does it usually act? How does it outside? Inside? And get to know the dog too. Talk to them, play with them, and spend more than just five minutes with them and remember that the way your shelter dog acts the first few weeks is not always how they will act. Give then time

Dr. Mike, the trick I and my family have always used when getting a pet from a shelter is let the pet come to you! Walk around and look at them all and whichever one seems the most interested in you, ask to have one on one time with them. If they still only want you, like they seek out your attention, want pets, are just all over you then chances are you're meant to have that pet. At least that's how it's worked out for us, been doing it all 40 of my years and I have yet to have a pet that hasn't meshed 100% with my family^^  Also! Introduce Bear to the new pet on neutral ground and bring them into your home together. Bear is a sweetheart but you don't want accidental territoriality to kick in^^

Most dog rescuers ask you stuff and cater to your needs :) There are however (sadly) some which are really "clicky" and cold-shoulder anyone who doesn't fit their "click" regardless if that person is perfect for rehoming a dog they have, with no regard for helping more dogs in the process. There are others which just exist for milking donation money - you know quickly who those are because they'll be background checking you for money and not just things like any criminal records with animals, how suitable your property/lifestyle is etc. It's understandable to be concerned someone may or may not be able to financially support a dog in the future, but its painfully obvious on their faces when you ask them how many animals they've rehomed in the last month and when you find out they have a hundred dogs in the back just sitting there to shake change from bleeding hearts. Run far, run fast - because these people even if you have the stamina/patience required to rehabilitate one and wrench a poor dog that's been distressed in a kennel for months on end, they will be pestering you for donations with guilt pics and videos forever!
If you truly want a cat, see if you can take Bear with you when choosing. Cats like to be in charge, and our dogs know and respect that (we have two of both dogs and cats). Bear seems real chill, he may accept a cat in charge. Our older dog took a little convincing, but now-a-days all get along fine. I would suggest you let them make friends under observation. A chill cat like Bear would be best, but you can never truly tell with a rescue what they are going to be like.

my family has always adopted rescues from shelters and I think the biggest & most important thing is to meet & spend time with the animal you're considering adopting. you'll get a chance to see their personality and it also gives them a chance to see yours. you should always talk to the staff & volunteers at a shelter too - they know the animal's history & where they came from and they also tend to know their personalities very well too since they work with them so much. I don't know if every shelter does this, but a lot of shelters in my area offer meet & greets if you already have a pet, so your current pet can meet whoever you're thinking about adopting. if a shelter offers it, I highly suggest taking advantage of it, but at the same time if they don't immediately get along it isn't the end of the world. sometimes they just get off on the wrong paw, and sometimes it really isn't going to work, so you need to pay attention & know the animals well I also highly encourage adopting an adult or a disabled animal. a lot of people tend to go for the patterned or healthy looking animals or the young energetic ones, and the elders & the disabled animals get left behind and end up spending way more time in the shelter. sometimes a disabled pet can be a lot of work depending on what their disability is, but it's very fulfilling and it's already hard enough for them to find a loving home as is. I have a cat who is missing an eye - there was a tiny bit of extra care we had to do when we first adopted him since he was still healing from surgery, but it was really only making sure the area stayed clean and taking him for his follow ups. adult or elderly animals are also still a great pet, some do have health issues or more medical care due to age but just like disabled pets they are still an amazing companion. rescued pets can always tell when you've saved them, and even though they can sometimes act a little crazy lol (like every pet), rescues always know you love them & they show their gratitude & love you right back. it's incredibly fulfilling and you save some amazing lives that way

I worked at a shelter in college and have owned rescue dogs my whole life. My advice would be to find a reputable rescue near you and have a chat with their adoption coordinator (some shelters don't always have the resources to have this position, but most rescues do). They match the dog to the lifestyle and housing situation, and if they don't have a dog to meet your specific needs then most will keep your information and give you a call when a potential new match comes in. Or if you have time, volunteer at a shelter and spend time with the dogs.

*TIPS for finding a dog that matches your personality*  PERFECT place is a shelter! I'd recommend no-kill shelters only tbh because I wouldn't want to support killing animals. It's bittersweet but true, so find some local no-kills near you and they usually let you take them out for a walk, some have a playpen to play with them or even meet your own dog! You can find puppies, adults, or senior dogs. They have them in all types so it's best to check frequently! Some even have websites to check their looks and personality. Then you can go meet them in real life and take them out for a test-run. Rescue dogs need no qualifications, which means their possibilities are limitless! Just make sure to grab the one you feel connected with most before someone else does. You can ALSO find cats that are good with dogs there! Rare, but possible. Dog adoption groups often have "adoption days" at  pet stores.  Look for those like at Petsmart, Petco, etc.  You get to visit with the dogs (and make sure to bring Bear along so he can visit, too) and if you find a dog that really vibes with you, you can fill out an application to adopt the dog.    However, you want to check with the organization because some require certain things at your home in order to adopt a dog.  You can also check with your local animal shelter and see what is required.  Some shelters have places where you can visit with the dogs and even bring your current dog to see if they get along.

Please consider rescuing a brown cat instead of buying.  In regards to finding a rescue dog that matches your personality type, most shelters do personality evaluations of varying degrees (some are in depth tests, others are what they see when they walk the dogs/take them outside for exercise).  If you talk to an employee/volunteer about what kind of pup you're looking for, they'd be happy to recommend specific dogs.  For the general person reading my comment: dogs shouldn't be grouped on personality based on what breed they are.  Like at the end of his video, Mike said a golden retriever attacked Bear at one point.  Yet Goldens are considered to be family dogs.  Breed temperament should be taken as an umbrella category rather than an expectation - cats and dogs are as vast in personalities as people are.  Its worse for dogs with bag stigma like pitbull breeds though.  Once considered a nanny dog, now considered a gangster owned child killer, or some other nonsense :/ yet the current "family dogs" (goldens, labs, poodles, corgis, shepherds, etc) are more dangerous bc people feel like they can get up in their space "because they are x breed and should be friendly."  My least favorite breed are German shepherds, or any big dog with a booming bark.  As someone who has worked in the vet field, GSDs and similar dogs are the most unpredictable.

We rescued all our pets. The best thing to do is talk to the shelter staff and be super honest about what you like to do with your dogs and what your homelife is like. I recommend vising with the dogs and volunteering to walk and play with them. If you find one you like, ask if you can bring Bear to play and go for a walk. Because shelters have a goal of keeping all animals in their forever homes. You don't want to bring home your new pup and have Bear or that dog have issues. You can even request to foster the dog for a few weeks to see if he/she connects with Bear. Most shelters will pay for vet bills and food during fostering. And if it does not work out you supported a dog in learning how to live in a house and follow rules, making them more adoptable and relieving pressure on over crowded shelters.
yes, you did in fact get a dog that looks somewhat like you. Where is the resemblance, you ask? It's definitely the hair.  Also be careful with your assumption of chillness in corgis... While Newfies like bear are bred just to be heavy lifters and companion animals, thus very chill and easygoing, corgis are a real working breed whose primary job was/is herding - they are extremely intelligent and easy to bore and can be quite a handful, prone to anxious herding behaviors like nipping. Honestly, you may find that little package of dog to be quite a bit more of a challenge than Bear. But considering you understand what husky energy is like, you would probably handle it fine. They're not the worst high energy herding breed either, but they're up there. You will be kept on your toes at some point!

You can still get one at the shelter. With my local Humane Society, and possibly most shelters, it isn't just a matter of going and picking a dog out of a cage and going home. You go and pick a dog, then you go out to a fenced area of the yard and spend a minimum of half an hour together. Then you come back another day and spend another half an hour with them. If you have other animals in your home you are encouraged to bring them for the second visit. Only after the dog has met every person living in the home at least once can you adopt the dog.

My corgi, Rosie is a rescue and she is the perfect dog for me. When you go to a humane society or shelter, let an employee know the size, energy level and other characteristics you want. The person at the front desk suggested Rosie after I told her what kind of dog I wanted and it ended up being a match made in heaven. You also have to trust your gut, but also spend time with any dog you have a really good feeling about. I was allowed to take her on a walk and spend time with her in a separate room so she was away from the sounds and noise of the adoption floor. The staff also gave me some toys for her to play with. After spending time with her I knew in my heart that she was meant to be my dog so I filled out the adoption application that day and the rest is history. She was estimated to be about 8 months old when I adopted her. She's now 9 and not a day goes by that I'm not thankful for her goofy personality and her snuggles. Corgis are herding dogs. Might not be a good choice if you are looking for a couch potato. Also, regarding finding a rescue, itâ€™s actually easier to find a rescue who fits with your personality than it is to purchase a puppy who will, because the rescue organization will have spent time with the dog and gotten to know it well. You donâ€™t actually know what you are getting personality-wise when you purchase a young puppy from a breeder or a pet store. You might have an idea of what the breedâ€™s temperament is like, and a breeder might tell you about the parents, but you donâ€™t know what the puppy itself will be like. A rescue, on the other hand, will usually be matched with you based on its temperament, personality, and behavior, so you have a much better chance at getting a good match with a rescue dog. I highly recommend reaching out to some local rescues in your area, as well as some breed specific rescues if you are looking for a certain breed. That way they can have your information on file should the perfect dog for you come into their care.

I rescued in a sense. I rehomed and I think it's the best middle ground of saving a life while also getting a dog breed or traits that fit you and your goals. So if a dog is common place enough (and even some that are rare may still pop up in rehome listings) you'll easily find them. Dogs like Dobermans, Huskies, German Shepherds, Malinois, Golden Retrievers, Labs, and many more are listed.   Rehoming is the process of a private adoption of a dog rather than giving it to a pound or a shelter. Sometimes an owner will reach out to an organization specialized in rehoming that breed. Rehoming is usually done when the owner has gone through a life change or realizes they can not provide for the animal as expected. A death in the family, finances, moving, or lack of time can all be possibilities. Usually the case is people will buy these dogs as puppies, and realize after 8-16 months they can't handle the dog and will rehome. Usually there is a rehoming fee but don't be dismayed. It's a good thing because it not only helps the owner recoup some financial loss but it also deters people who may want to get a dog but are not serious about it's care or would rehome the dog for nefarious reasons. It shouldn't be crazy like $2000 but expect like $1000 or less (usually less).  The way to find these dogs that need new homes would be Facebook and a quick google search. Two years ago I realized a standard poodle fit my needs and goals but I knew buying a dog was not something I wanted to do nor was financially capable (broke college kid). That's when I scoured Facebook, Facebook groups, and online websites. Eventually I found a lady in buttfuck nowhere Arkansas. Your best bet would be to find a breed of dog you like and want. Then go to Facebook and query. Type in "poodle rehome" then "poodle home" then "poodle new home"; anything that may pull up a search result where someone has made a post about being heart broken and needing to give up their pooch. You can also look up on Fabecook "poodle rehome group" or anything along those lines because people will join those groups and post when they need to rehome or know someone that needs to rehome a dog. With Facebook definitely do your due diligence with talking to the owner, asking questions, and figuring out what was this dog's past life. Especially ask for you vet records, and if the vaccines weren't administered by a certified vet (the dog breeder can administer it or even shop owners of farming/pet stores can do it - I made this mistake and my poodle caught parvo) just take a note of that and get all the vaccines again by your vet. Also ask is the dog has any training, commands, and is crate and potty trained. Ask what the dog's energy and temperament is like too. This is important because when it comes to lifestyle and training it'll be difficult to retrain a dog or break bad habits over getting a "green" dog (training term for a dog with little to 0 training at all).  This post is long but do holla at me if you're interested. I went through this with my standard poodle and there are mistakes I've made and lessons I learned that I would love to tell people. I think this is the best middle ground between buying and saving and it really is a sick opportunity. You're helping yourself, helping the previous owner knowing their dog is going to a good home, and you're helping the dog. It's a great opportunity!

This may be a repeat of other comments, but I've always adopted rescues - specifically senior dogs are my passion - and there are usually advocacy groups that work with shelters as well as fosters. Of course you can go to a shelter and meet dogs, and many times you can set up and meet n greet at the shelter with a dog of intereat and take Bear to see how they get along. I've found that fosters are one of the best ways to get to know a dog, because dogs act very differently in a busy kennel vs a home. If a dog is being fostered, just reach out to the foster and ask about their personality and let them know what your home is like and what you're looking for in a dog, and the foster can tell you if they believe it will be a good fit. It can take some time and meeting lots of pups, but it is so worth it! So many rescue dogs are absolutely wonderful pets. Good luck on that search when the time comes!

I just got a rescue dog 3 weeks ago tomorrow! Her name is Izzy and she was NOT the dog I originally went with! I wanted a dog named Molly, but the shelter advised that based on what I said I was looking for/my current situation, she might not be a good fit. I asked for them to point me in the right direction, and they came back with 3 dogs that I looked into, and that's how I found Izzy. We have a lot of work to do, and she has some issues, and she doesn't really know how to be a dog, and I get the feeling that she really went through it before she made it to me, but I already can't imagine my life without her. Even before our trial week was over I knew that the only way they were getting her away from me was to pry her away.  Point is, if you want a shelter dog and don't know how to make sure the two of you mesh, ask the shelter to play matchmaker. They're really good at it.

In my experience, dogs do remember the bad things that happen to them. My Dad had his blue heeler x red kelpie, Chip, for sixteen years. When Chip was about three years old, an extended family member's kid threw a rock at him. For the rest of his life, if that kid tried to lead him around or anything like that, he would growl at them. He was nothing but sweet, patient and cuddly with everyone else. On a lighter note, Chip met one particularly kind member of my family twice in his life; first as a puppy, then again at the age of fourteen. At the second meeting, he ran straight to her, and greeted her like an old friend. She was very touched. So they also remember the good things. ğŸ˜Š

As someone thatâ€™s family has rescued a dog, let me tell you about Pam. My mom lost twins, and then the dog my dad got for my mom to try and cheer her up died from some illness picked up at the shelter. So after that bit of morbidness, in 1999 my mom and dad went to the shelter. All the dogs were barking so loudly at my parents. Except one. In the far back corner, there was this tiny little puppy, and she was a border collie. They said she had one week until she would be put down. So my parents adopted her. She comforted my mom, was great for my sisters, and then little baby me came the next year. I had my best friend from the minute I was brought home from the hospital, until I looked in her beautiful eyes, holding her paw, telling her hold much I loved her while they injected the medication to let her pass. Trust me. Get to know the dog at a local shelter, see if they have an area where you can take them outside in a gated area (at least the shelter at mine does) and ask about the dogs history if there is any.

To rescue a dog that fits your lifestyle, contact a rescue that foster dogs in people's homes instead of keeping them in kennel. To meet a rescue dog for you it's important to go visit the shelters and hang out with the animals. Take the dogs out of the kennels and hang out one on one and see their true personality. When they are in the kennels they can be scared or act aggressive. You can also most likely depending on where you are at foster or do home trials on a dog or cat you are interested in to see how they interact with Bear. Just make sure not to judge the dogs while they are in the kennels because their personalities change drastically once you get them outside and even more once they get home and relax and don't have to be on edge.

Rescue tips! I grew up with a bunch of rescue and foster dogs and this is my experience. Iâ€™d start talking to and building a relationship with a local rescue organization so that they can help you connect with a dog that will be a good fit in your home. It might take time for the right dog to pop up, but itâ€™s worth the wait to hold out for the right one, because rescues can be just the sweetest most perfect addition to your household! A good rescue should also do a little background on you and do a meeting between the two dogs before sealing the deal. Sometimes they might have a dog in a foster situation whoâ€™s ready to start transitioning, and other times you might have to wait a bit. The other option is to try fostering, which can be a big emotional drain but gives dogs a place to be while waiting on their forever home, and eventually you could find the dog that is your â€œfoster failâ€ and stays with you forever!

Tip for Doctor Mike: You find your perfect rescue by meeting them. They know, and they will tell you. I used to think that was BS. Then it happened with me and my dog and I thought it was a once-in-a-lifetime occurrence. But since then I've seen it happen over and over. The dog just knows. I've seen the most stubborn, ridiculous hound become a perfect angel the moment he met his forever family. A shy, timid momma's boy instantly come out of his shell when meeting his new parents. It's so hard to describe, but it's like they've known you all their life and have just been waiting for you the whole time.  If I had gone looking by *just* my set of criteria, I wouldn't have considered my dog. Sure, I had certain things I needed like grooming and size limits. But then I met her and she turned out to be my perfect companion. She just knew. I'm so grateful for the 14 years I got to spend with her. When you go to meet a new dog, listen to them. They will know if they are yours, and they will tell you.

Mike!!! I have a rescue dog, she is absolutely bonkers! There are some things I couldn't help her with, difficult fears that she still struggles with even after all the trainning, so we kinda learned to live with it as it is. I feel rescues have other kind of needs, yet she is cleverer than other dogs I had, as well as thankful and extremelly happy just "to be there" (literally she smiles and wags her tail just because I decided to open a window or something). However, I didn't get her by specifically looking that we matched. I've meet different dogs, and I turned to the "problematic" area, which was part of the rescue shelter where they had dogs that bite, bark, are sick, or very afraid. She lived with other dog and I played with that dog whereas she hid in the corner shaking. I thought the dog I was playing with would have more opportunities (she did, she was adopted two weeks after!) than the dog shaking, which was the one I took home. We didn't understand each other at all the first days, but I adopted her during summer so I had time to work through it). Right now I am the one who has sepparation anxiety, she takes care of my panic attacks, she learned where my asthma inhaler is and understands looks and signs as full commands mostly because our bond grow unbeatable. What I am trying to say is that in some cases, the bond is something that needs time, care and hard work, but when you finally have it is super precious. My dog grew up with other dog I had, a big big oldie that ate even the cookbooks, and he was the perfect lazy mentor ever. If you decide to get a rescue, I bet Bear would teach them every naughty trick. However, they are hard work even if you get them young and even if you have experience training dogs. Any animal we adopt, we meet first. And then we meet a few more and go based on those interactions. So many people see a cute picture online and just go get that dog/cat. We always do our research before going in, and then we ask a lot of questions at the shelter to not only be sure it's the right addition to our family, but also that we are right for the new addition. Both my fiance and I are allergic to cats, but some are worse than others (I'm sure it's the dander). We make sure we spend enough time with each cat that we'd hit our allergy cues or not. The last thing we'd want to do is take home and have to bring back to shelter. So much stress for the poor animal. We also have a 4 year old. We make sure she's there to meet the animal as well to see if they can handle her excitement and energy (she loves to play and hold them, and can have her loud moments). If we have another animal at the time, we have them meet. Of course there will be an introduction period at home if needed, but it's nice to get an initial reaction as well. For example, our dog is good with some dogs, but not others. If you get a cat (or another dog), just make sure you ask if they know if the new animal gets along with others and see if they will let them meet (not all places will allow).

I used to volunteer at a shelter and I think the best advice I can give for adopting a rescue is to visit and play with them (dog) a couple times to make sure your personalities align. THEN bring Bear and introduce them at the shelter. Most places have a little fenced in area for this. The dog introduction is the MOST important step that some folks often forget about and end up returning the dog when they realize it wasnâ€™t a good match. Also donâ€™t be afraid to return the dog if it doesnâ€™t work out. Us volunteers ALWAYS preferred this to you & the doggos living in misery.

I'm a dog trainer in training (weird I know) but I've trained over 5 show dogs. The reason Bear knows which floor to get off is your body language. Even if you aren't aware, your body makes adjustments and prepares to leave the elevator before the doors even open. Those slight things are indications for Bear to prepare for command (moving, walking, sitting, ect.) Your dog is very intuitive to you. It's actually a sign that he's constantly waiting for your cues.

Quote of the day "Do they make brown cats"
Your concern about finding the right rescue dog is very common. The good news is that shelters and rescues (as far as I have seen) have people called adoption counselors. These people know the available dogs' personalities and needs and can match you with the right one based on what you share about your needs/wants/lifestyle. If none of the pups are the right fit, they may also provide a service where they record the info they've learned about you and then contact you when the right doggo comes through. If you are unsure that a dog is right for you, some shelters offer foster to adopt programs where the dog gets a break from the shelter and you get to see exactly what it's like to have them in your home. Also don't feel the need to adopt the first time you visit the shelter. Your perfect match might be there on your first visit, but they might not. Be patient, the right doggo is worth waiting for. And be prepared to need to do some reprogramming with your new pup. I'm by no means saying that shelter dogs automatically come with issues and bad habits, but I feel like most dogs aren't the perfect goodboy you have raised Bear to be. It is a very rewarding and well worth it process, just be prepared to need a little extra patience and need to spend a little extra time with the new addition. The shelter should be able to help you through the transition. That being said, many shelter animals are there through no fault of their own and have amazing socialization and training. I've had multiple shelter cats who have been perfect angels from the second they came home.

I'm in Toronto so it would take a trip but I got my rescue from Coveted Canines and they were super great at matching the dog to my circumstances. I have chronic pain and live in a tiny apartment so they matched me with my sweet Chihuahua mix (Mr. Darcy) who is incredibly quiet for a Chi mix and a snuggle butt who motivates me to get just enough exercise to keep myself healthy without aggravating my pain. Coveted Canines specializes in rescuing dogs from kill shelters (Darcy had three days to live which is bonkers to me because he's such a good dog) so you can feel good about rescuing a buddy who wouldn't have made it otherwise. Anyways, getting a rescue from Canada that was rescued from the USA or Mexico (kill shelters are mostly illegal here) might be a bit complicated so my advice is to check if the rescue you are looking at has a Facebook or Instagram or something so you can read what the clients have to say about the process.

One of the most important things to finding a rescue dog that suits you is consider the increase in pet expenses, pet 1-1 time/management, and the 3-3-3 rule. 3 days of feeling nervous and adjusting to the change/shift in environment/rules/scheduling 3 weeks of settling into the new home and making a full environmental adjustment 3 months of building a trust and bond with you Of course there are exceptions to this rule such as the new pet has violent rather than aggressive behaviors and risks immediate harm to you or other pets. I always hope adopters will give the new pet at least 7 days. But a responsible shelter will have behavior tested their dogs for food or toy aggression(possessiveness) and marked them as [only pet] dogs. This doesn't necessarily mean they're bad dogs, because all dogs can be worked out of certain behaviors, but with how many pets cycle through shelters and considering most all of them arrive without background knowledge, the shelter doesn't have the time to invest in 1-1 training for each pet. A responsible shelter will also have both dogs meet somewhere that isn't your home prior to introducing the new pet to you and Bear's home. Regardless of if Bear is dog-friendly, the other dog may have anxieties when entering a home covered completely in the scent of another dog. This may cause them to behave differently than expected, but should be easy fixes if behavior is corrected ASAP.  Most of these things can be applied to a shelter cat if you end up sticking to that wager. I didn't mean to ramble, but I'm pretty adamant about giving shelter pets a good name and encouraging #AdoptDontShop   Good luck! Love you Bear!
