Hey Doctor Mike, I know you live in the states and I'm from Ontario, Canada but I'm hopeful that you have a similar organization near you. When adopting our rescues, we visited our local shelters (ASPCA I think its called down south) but had our best success with a place called Dog Tales in King City, Canada.  They rescue dogs from around the world, including from the Miami-Dade shelter in Florida.  To adopt from them requires a bit of time on the person's part as they make sure the dog will be happy and successful in your life.  I'm sure similar organizations exist in your home state, that can provide a similar adopting experience. Its very important to get to know the rescue through multiple visits, walks and by speaking with the dog's trainer and/or foster-person about their personalities. Its so sad when people bring a rescue to their homes based, usually on the dog's looks or breed, then return them to the shelter a day, week, month, or year in the case of my husky mix later, because they didn't behave as anticipated. Breed makes up part of how they might behave, however their past experiences and individual personalities are more of an indicator of how they will fit into your world. When you are planning on getting a fur friend for Bear, I'm sure you will bring Bear for his input on each prospective candidate. Wishing you and Bear a healthy,  happy 2023. ğŸ¤—ğŸ‡¨ğŸ‡¦

I've adopted a few rescue dogs and now volunteer as a foster (and just joined the board!) of a nonprofit animal rescue. I think the best way to find a good fit for you and Bear is to find a rescue that keeps their animals in foster homes, so you get a much better sense of their personality and quirks vs. in a kennel environment where most dogs are stressed out. You can do meet & greets with the foster & rescue dog with Bear to see how they interact and even try a couple home visits. Most rescue dogs need a few weeks to settle into their new home, but most are so happy to be in a safe place they end up being great dogs!

I've been volunteering with an animal rescue group for ~25 years. My number one tip for finding the right pet -- dog, cat, etc -- is to find a rescue group that keeps their pets in foster homes. That way, you can talk to someone who has lived with that pet and knows what they're like in a home environment. Also, see if you can do a trial visit with the pet you like. Our rescue does what we call a babysitting period: the pet stays with you for two weeks, so you can make sure it's the right fit. If at any time you decide it isn't, we take the pet back and you can try a different one (or decide not to adopt at all, no pressure). But if you decide that it's the perfect fit, you fill out the adoption paperwork and pay the adoption fee then.   We're also always available if our adopters need advice. And we encourage them to contact us for help sooner rather than later. It's easier to fix a problem when it first starts than it is if it's been going on for months.

OMG I think Dr. Mike would be a great cat dad, as long as he understands 6 things:  1) DON'T TOUCH THE BELLY! Cats show their bellies to people as a sign of trust, not because they want you to touch their bellies. Only touch their bellies if you want to get scratched[or you have a cat that actually likes belly rubs].  2) Dogs wag their tails when they're happy, cats wag/thrash their tails when they're agitated. That's when you back off and leave them alone otherwise you will get scratched[or bit].  3) Open mouth breathing in cats is considered an emergency because healthy cats only breathe through their noses. If a cat is breathing through its mouth it means that it's either sick or extremely stressed.  4) cats are crepuscular, not nocturnal. Crepuscular means they're most active at dawn and dusk. Also cats have pro-tractable claws, not retractable claws.  5) Declawing is cruel and inhumane. It shouldn't be called declawing, it's actually "de-knuckling" the cat. And it will make them more aggressive because you took away their primary defensive weapon.  6) Cats are obligate carnivores, meaning they need meat to survive, and as such they cannot live on a vegan diet. Even so-called "vegan cat food" that is supplemented with Taurine and other essential nutrients isn't good enough because a cats body[from its teeth to its digestive system] has evolved to living on a meat based diet and simply cannot digest large amounts of plant material.

As someone who's had shelter dogs her entire life: A lot of good shelters and organizations will be happy to work with you in searching your next companion.  I reach out to them, explain my situation and what I'm looking for in a dog (before I even go and see the dogs they have!) and then they will either suggest a dog who will match with what I've described or not. A lot of organizations also work with foster families and they can provide an even greater in-depth picture of their dog's personality.   When you get a puppy from a breeder; even the best breeders in the world; you also wouldn't know what their personalities will be like, right? With shelter dogs, you will already get somewhat of an estimation like wether or not they are friendly with other dogs or if they have separation anxiety or if they have medical issues, etc.   I would strongly advise everyone N O T to go and check out the dogs in their kennels beforehand. I really wouldn't. Why? Because it is a stressful environment for these dogs and they will absolutely not be acting the way they truly are.  (Edit: Loudly barking dogs are likely just stressed out and so don't give a good first impression and are therefore not given a fair chance! After all, how many of you have fantastic family dogs who also bark at complete strangers approaching their territory when they're by themselves...?)   Best to find a match by talking to the staff and ask if you can meet the dog in a separate room; as most shelters do have that option.

To answer your question you asked at 10:43 about finding a rescue dog, there are programs known as foster-to-adopt which means that you can foster a rescue dog for a period of time with the intention to adopt but if it doesn't work out, you don't have to go through with the adoption and you  continue to foster until the dog finds a different home.  An organization that I've fostered for is the NYCACC (New York City Animal Care Center) and they have shelters in Manhattan, Brooklyn and Staten Island! They have lots of dogs of all kinds looking for a forever home and they always need fosters as they are an open intake shelter! Sorry for the long comment. Hope this helps!

I have major depressive disorder, with very little motivation other than playing games and watching videos on my phone and laptop.
